<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_318952179">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/318952179" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="left imgcol" title="Lyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428721461p2/5253785.jpg" alt="Lyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/318952179?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 26, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="user" title="Lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer318952179">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9810065551998420258">Citizen of the Galaxy by Robert A, Heinlein was first published in 1957 and is generally considered one of his juvenile novels as Scribner’s published it. <br /><br />An astute reader of Heinlein, though, may consider that this was published just a year before <a title="Have Space Suit—Will Travel by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20417.Have_Space_Suit_Will_Travel">Have Space Suit—Will Travel</a>, the last of the Scribner’s juveniles, in the same year as <a title="The Door Into Summer by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/348.The_Door_Into_Summer">The Door Into Summer</a> and only four years before <a title="Stranger in a Strange Land by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/350.Stranger_in_a_Strange_Land">Stranger in a Strange Land</a>, so his transition from the more typical pure science bildungsroman of his earlier works</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9810065551998420258">Citizen of the Galaxy by Robert A, Heinlein was first published in 1957 and is generally considered one of his juvenile novels as Scribner’s published it. <br /><br />An astute reader of Heinlein, though, may consider that this was published just a year before <a title="Have Space Suit—Will Travel by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20417.Have_Space_Suit_Will_Travel">Have Space Suit—Will Travel</a>, the last of the Scribner’s juveniles, in the same year as <a title="The Door Into Summer by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/348.The_Door_Into_Summer">The Door Into Summer</a> and only four years before <a title="Stranger in a Strange Land by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/350.Stranger_in_a_Strange_Land">Stranger in a Strange Land</a>, so his transition from the more typical pure science bildungsroman of his earlier works and a more mature, serious work of the sixties had begun. <br /><br />Without a doubt, Heinlein reached his zenith in the sixties with <a title="The Moon is a Harsh Mistress by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16690.The_Moon_is_a_Harsh_Mistress">The Moon is a Harsh Mistress</a>, Stranger in a Strange Land and <a title="Starship Troopers by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17214.Starship_Troopers">Starship Troopers</a> (actually published in 1959). Citizen of the Galaxy could be seen as an early embarkation towards these classics.<br /><br />Telling the tale of Thorby and his journeys as a slave, a beggar, a trader, a soldier and finally as a tycoon, Heinlein uses the tale as a vehicle to explore many futuristic ideas still grounded in everyday life. Heinlein succeeds as he has in so many of his works, by creating an entertaining story while at the same time using the science fiction as an allegory to describe his libertarian ideals about a great many subjects such as family relationships and dynamics, freedom, work ethics, and loyalty. <br /><br />Slavery is a central subject of the novel and Heinlein spends his time well with some provocative dialogue. At the end of the day, Heinlein is a great writer simply because he tells a good story. Give him that he provokes thought from his reader and that the story works well on more than one level, and Heinlein has once again delivered a good read.<br /><br /><img width="400" height="173" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451142555i/17511192._SX540_.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9810065551998420258">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_27081054">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27081054" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/970517-katharine" class="left imgcol" title="Katharine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1204774103p2/970517.jpg" alt="Katharine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27081054?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 12, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/970517-katharine" class="user" title="Katharine">Katharine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/970517-katharine?shelf=scifi" class="actionLinkLite">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27081054">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1270789777714285780">Some of my all-time favorite sci-fi novels were written by Robert Heinlein. Some of the worst, stupidest, most incoherent, hipper-than-thou sci-fi novels were also written by Robert Heinlein. So every time I check out a Heinlein from the library, it is with great trepidation. <br /><br />I am happy to say that <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> is one of the first category – a good Heinlein. It is creative, likeable, possibly even inspiring. The hero, Thorby, is one of his better characters. A lot of things happen to hi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1270789777714285780">Some of my all-time favorite sci-fi novels were written by Robert Heinlein. Some of the worst, stupidest, most incoherent, hipper-than-thou sci-fi novels were also written by Robert Heinlein. So every time I check out a Heinlein from the library, it is with great trepidation. <br /><br />I am happy to say that <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> is one of the first category – a good Heinlein. It is creative, likeable, possibly even inspiring. The hero, Thorby, is one of his better characters. A lot of things happen to him, but he manages to walk the fine line between comic book superhero and passive victim, while escaping the kind of brash obnoxiousness that sometimes afflicts Heinlein heros. The minor characters are fairly well done too. <br /><br />When Heinlein really gets it right, he is one of the best writers I know at creating fascinating settings, cultures, and societies. This is true in <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em>. Like many of his novels, there's a political bent to the ending of the book, but he doesn't draw it out too much, and ends the book quickly but satisfyingly. I have no idea how he does it, but he even manages to make stock transactions fairly interesting. <br /><br />The plot is pretty coherent with a nice arc to it. It does get a little uneven in places, devoting too much time to details and skipping too quickly over large sections of events the author is less interested in, but which could have been instrumental in really capturing the reader. Again, rambling plots and unevenness of pacing are some of Heinlein's biggest flaws when he's at his worst, but he manages to hold it together here. <br /><br />I wouldn't rate this as high as my all-time fav Heinlein, <em>
  <a title=" The Door into Summer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Door%20into%20Summer"> The Door into Summer</a>
</em>, but it's pretty near my second, <em>
  <a title=" The Moon is a Harsh Mistress" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Moon%20is%20a%20Harsh%20Mistress"> The Moon is a Harsh Mistress</a>
</em>. I recommend it, even for non-Heinlein-fans. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1270789777714285780">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1210335501">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1210335501" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin" class="left imgcol" title="Mike (the Paladin)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450193915p2/2187043.jpg" alt="Mike (the Paladin)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1210335501?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin" class="user" title="Mike (the Paladin)">Mike (the Paladin)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18201772-citizen-of-the-galaxy" title="Audio CD 9781482947205" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2187043-mike-the-paladin?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1210335501">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10135037084688104249">Somehow I had managed (over the decades) to miss this Heinlein novel. It like so many others is in many ways a masterpiece. We begin with a young boy who's a slave. Hie memories of who he was before his slavery is essentially nil. On the block he still shows some spirit, enough to get him cuffed. <br /><br />But he doesn't sell. Being young, small and scrawny no one seems willing to put in the time and money it would take to train him up into a useful slave... No one buys him that is until a local beggar bu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10135037084688104249">Somehow I had managed (over the decades) to miss this Heinlein novel. It like so many others is in many ways a masterpiece. We begin with a young boy who's a slave. Hie memories of who he was before his slavery is essentially nil. On the block he still shows some spirit, enough to get him cuffed. <br /><br />But he doesn't sell. Being young, small and scrawny no one seems willing to put in the time and money it would take to train him up into a useful slave... No one buys him that is until a local beggar buys him.<br /><br />We will follow this "person" through the years and as we do we'll find an interesting story concerning the Galaxy and the humans therein.<br /><br />This is considered (by most anyway) to be one of Heinlein's youth or teen books. However the subject matter and the story telling is on an adult level and I don't think any science fiction fan who tries this one will be disappointed. <br /><br />So, I've said what I have to say. I like it, I recommend it, enjoy. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10135037084688104249">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_226195837">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226195837" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar" class="left imgcol" title="Dirk Grobbelaar"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1438762505p2/3102740.jpg" alt="Dirk Grobbelaar" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/226195837?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 31, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar" class="user" title="Dirk Grobbelaar">Dirk Grobbelaar</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1090853.Citizen_Of_The_Galaxy" title=" 9780140470710" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=books-i-own" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer226195837">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer901009846028529595">Highly regarded as one of the best of Heinlein’s juvies, <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> is indeed, for the most part, worthy of the praise that has been heaped upon it. I do have one qualm with this novel; it seems to lack a nemesis, or antagonist. There is quite a bit that happens – but to what end? There are no “bad guys” so it’s a bit bland at times. The slavers, and slavery, are the closest the novel ever comes to real conflict, but that is handled in such a peripheral fashion that there is never any</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText901009846028529595">Highly regarded as one of the best of Heinlein’s juvies, <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> is indeed, for the most part, worthy of the praise that has been heaped upon it. I do have one qualm with this novel; it seems to lack a nemesis, or antagonist. There is quite a bit that happens – but to what end? There are no “bad guys” so it’s a bit bland at times. The slavers, and slavery, are the closest the novel ever comes to real conflict, but that is handled in such a peripheral fashion that there is never any real sense of threat. In the end, the novel turns into a courtroom and boardroom drama. This is connected to the overlying plot, but the coincidences involved are a bit hard to swallow. <br /><br />On the other hand, this novel succeeds fairly well as a bildungsroman and as a science fiction novel aimed at younger readers. There is almost no violence and the scientific expositions are fairly straightforward and interesting. There is a lot of allegory and Heinlein gets positively preachy about the importance of family and owning up to responsibility.<br /><br />So where does that leave me? It isn’t a <em>bad</em> novel by <strong>any</strong> definition, but it did leave me feeling a bit unsatisfied. Perhaps I’ve just become desensitized…<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="901009846028529595">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/43737942?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 24, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1935318-ann" class="user" title="Ann">Ann</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer43737942">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4875631853158655186">I've read a LOT of Heinlein, and this book doesn't read as much like a "typical" Heinlein book as others I've read. The main character is very serious --yes, he was a slave, but usually Heinlein books involve a certain witty dialogue that this character lacked.<br /><br />That said, it was still an amazing book. We meet Thorby as he's being unloaded from a slave ship, and follow him through his life from there on. He's adopted by a begger/slave trade fighter in disguise, Baslim, who he calls Pop. From there</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4875631853158655186">I've read a LOT of Heinlein, and this book doesn't read as much like a "typical" Heinlein book as others I've read. The main character is very serious --yes, he was a slave, but usually Heinlein books involve a certain witty dialogue that this character lacked.<br /><br />That said, it was still an amazing book. We meet Thorby as he's being unloaded from a slave ship, and follow him through his life from there on. He's adopted by a begger/slave trade fighter in disguise, Baslim, who he calls Pop. From there, Thorby goes on to be adopted into the complicated "family" of a Free Trade ship, who's captain is fulfilling an oath to Baslim by adopting Thorby. We learn all about the Free Traders through an anthropologist on the ship who is studying them, and I found this break down of their culture very interesting. Thorby is then transferred into the hands of the military, as part of the Captain's oath to Baslim-- find out who Thorby really is. <br /><br />Begin Thorby's stint serving in the military, gaining status because of his arms defense knowledge learned from the traders. Eventually we find out who he really is, and who Baslim really was. The rest of the book is finished on Earth, where Thorby finally fulfills his destiny, and his legacy to Pop. <br /><br />As this book was ending, I kept looking at the remaining pages and thinking "no WAY this is going to resolve itself in 10, 5, 2 pages!!". And I was right, it didn't. The end of the book left me wanting more, as I don't feel it fully wrapped up the story. Aside from this, <u>Citizen Of The Galexy</u> was a great read!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4875631853158655186">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_329708069">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329708069" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish" class="left imgcol" title="Simon Mcleish"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347099463p2/7915393.jpg" alt="Simon Mcleish" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/329708069?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 14, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish" class="user" title="Simon Mcleish">Simon Mcleish</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7915393-simon-mcleish?shelf=owned" class="actionLinkLite">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer329708069">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2566302945361562057">Originally published on my blog <a rel="nofollow" href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/1999/08/robert-heinlein-citizen-of-galaxy-1957.html">here</a> in August 1999.<br /><br />At one stage of his career, Heinlein wrote a series of novels aimed at what is now termed the "young adult" market; Citizen of the Galaxy is one of the best of these. This is partly because it has something of a message yet is still entertaining escapism.<br /><br />The moral is hardly a revolutionary one; it has been pretty generally accepted throughout the twentieth century. It can be summed up as "slavery is evil", and though mainly concerned with slave</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2566302945361562057">Originally published on my blog <a rel="nofollow" href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/1999/08/robert-heinlein-citizen-of-galaxy-1957.html">here</a> in August 1999.<br /><br />At one stage of his career, Heinlein wrote a series of novels aimed at what is now termed the "young adult" market; Citizen of the Galaxy is one of the best of these. This is partly because it has something of a message yet is still entertaining escapism.<br /><br />The moral is hardly a revolutionary one; it has been pretty generally accepted throughout the twentieth century. It can be summed up as "slavery is evil", and though mainly concerned with slavery as traditionally practised, it contains rather subtler references to extend the idea of slavery to cover any life determined by involuntary rules imposed from outside. The central character, Thorby, basically passes through several different sorts of 'slavery' - ownership by another, membership of a ritually constrained culture, the discipline imposed by the armed forces. (I am not sure that Heinlein would have considered this a form of slavery when enlistment is voluntary.)<br /><br />The plot of the novel is fairly implausible. Thorby, a slave for as long as he can remember, is sold at the market on the planet Sargon VIII, part of a group of planets (the Imperium) where slavery is an important social institution. He is bought - at a knock down price - by an old beggar, Baslim, and adopted as his son. Baslim is not what he appears to be; he turns out to be a spy dedicated to eradicating slavery. When suspected by the Imperium he is killed; but Thorby escapes with the help of the Free Traders Baslim had used as couriers for his messages.<br /><br />The Free Traders are basically spacegoing merchant princes, who have subordinated their entire lives to ritual designed to maximise profit and make it possible for a large community to live in the restricted environment of a spaceship. When Thorby finds this new slavery constricting, he is able to join the armed forces of the Terran Hegemony, in which Baslim had been an important officer. The identity check performed on new recruits then causes the most implausible twist in the book: Thorby is actually Thor Rudbek, heir to one of Earth's biggest corporate fortunes, presumed dead after his parents' space yacht went missing when he was a baby.<br /><br />The implausibility is hidden by skilful writing; the characters are believable' and this makes it one of Heinlein's best books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2566302945361562057">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8868118">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14933037070401357469"><a title="Citizen of the Galaxy by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/540493.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy">Citizen of the Galaxy</a> is a throw back to the days when a complete story could be told in 250 pages or less. A nice 3 Star diversion to a simpler time in SciFi, when "atomics" were all the rage and everyone had them, strictly for defense you know. Heinlein tells a tale of a young lad, sold as a slave to an old beggar--who is not what he seems. The boy grows and has adventures as he goes on a mission for the old beggar that will journey through several different cultures. Heinlein paints 3 differe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14933037070401357469"><a title="Citizen of the Galaxy by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/540493.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy">Citizen of the Galaxy</a> is a throw back to the days when a complete story could be told in 250 pages or less. A nice 3 Star diversion to a simpler time in SciFi, when "atomics" were all the rage and everyone had them, strictly for defense you know. Heinlein tells a tale of a young lad, sold as a slave to an old beggar--who is not what he seems. The boy grows and has adventures as he goes on a mission for the old beggar that will journey through several different cultures. Heinlein paints 3 different cultures in full enough detail in just a few pages. Very enjoyable. Well worth a couple of hours -- that is all it will take.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_428539731">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/428539731" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5792975-francis" class="left imgcol" title="Francis"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1310594162p2/5792975.jpg" alt="Francis" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/428539731?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5792975-francis" class="user" title="Francis">Francis</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/540493.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345260741" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer428539731">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer542008840711144067">It just took me fifty years to finish this book.<br /><br />Yes, it's true, I started this when I was about nine or ten. I had checked it out from the library and I was immediately immersed in the story of the young slave bought by a crippled beggar. But, the life of a ten year old got in the way. And, I had to play baseball, and I had to learn commerce, which I did by trading, marbles, baseball cards, stamps and comics and learned the painful but necessary lessons of childhood like never trade a puree for</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText542008840711144067">It just took me fifty years to finish this book.<br /><br />Yes, it's true, I started this when I was about nine or ten. I had checked it out from the library and I was immediately immersed in the story of the young slave bought by a crippled beggar. But, the life of a ten year old got in the way. And, I had to play baseball, and I had to learn commerce, which I did by trading, marbles, baseball cards, stamps and comics and learned the painful but necessary lessons of childhood like never trade a puree for less than five cat-eyes, and never, ever trust a sixth grader. <br /><br />My time ran out and I had to return the book or otherwise face the wrath of my father who would not tolerate money spent on expired library books, no matter how reasonable the fines. My family had a lot of pride and paying fines was considered shameful.<br /><br />Anyway I remembered the title and the author, but sadly the book was always checked out, whenever I returned to the library.<br /><br />But then many years later (like now) in a nostalgic mood I found an e-book version and decided to finally finish what I had set out to do as a child.<br /><br />Well, I still enjoyed the part about the young slave and the crippled beggar. But, when it got around to the end with Heinlein trying to explain corporate behavior to young juveniles while sermonizing on freedom and personal responsibility? <br /><br />It got me to thinking that as a child I was lucky to have left off with only a memory of a young slave adopted by a wise and kindly beggar.<br /><br />It was like having the good Heinlein without the bizarre parts. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="542008840711144067">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/2954212?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 11, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16958-dan" class="user" title="Dan">Dan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of science fiction</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2954212">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1943284316479530797">The story of a slave boy who becomes free and grows up, making his way through the Galaxy.<br /><br />This is a thoroughly enjoyable science fiction book.<br /><br />There isn't much character development, which is a little lame, because it is a story of a young man growing up.<br /><br />However, the Heinlein's galaxy provides an interesting allegory for many human institutions such as government, free trade, education, and slavery.<br /><br />The anti-slavery argument presented in this novel is more than just a condemnation of slavery as "r</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1943284316479530797">The story of a slave boy who becomes free and grows up, making his way through the Galaxy.<br /><br />This is a thoroughly enjoyable science fiction book.<br /><br />There isn't much character development, which is a little lame, because it is a story of a young man growing up.<br /><br />However, the Heinlein's galaxy provides an interesting allegory for many human institutions such as government, free trade, education, and slavery.<br /><br />The anti-slavery argument presented in this novel is more than just a condemnation of slavery as "racist." Slavery in the galaxy is not racially based. Rather, the anti-slavery philosophy presented argues from an individualistic point of view; it argues that slavery is wrong because it is a violation of personal rights by the government and economic concerns.<br /></span>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1211774977">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1211774977" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9784757-hannah" class="left imgcol" title="Hannah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1421728256p2/9784757.jpg" alt="Hannah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1211774977?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9784757-hannah" class="user" title="Hannah">Hannah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1211774977">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6889708432552820940">This book was incredibly jarring. I felt like Robert Heinlein had a good idea...then he had another one...then he had another one. And since he liked all three of them, he decided "What the Hell!" and combined them. The book features a boy named Thorby. At the beginning of the book, he is sold as a slave to a beggar man. The beggar man (surprise) turns out to be more then he appears. He trains up Thorby and turns him into a super smart and clever beggar.<br /><br />Then he is murdered. We don't know why. I'</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6889708432552820940">This book was incredibly jarring. I felt like Robert Heinlein had a good idea...then he had another one...then he had another one. And since he liked all three of them, he decided "What the Hell!" and combined them. The book features a boy named Thorby. At the beginning of the book, he is sold as a slave to a beggar man. The beggar man (surprise) turns out to be more then he appears. He trains up Thorby and turns him into a super smart and clever beggar.<br /><br />Then he is murdered. We don't know why. I'm serious. I finished the book and I don't know why he died. During the book they make it sound like it was part of a conspiracy, but then they just never get around to explaining what that is. Doesn't matter, Thorby stows away on a ship and part 2 of the book has begun!<br /><br />Now he becomes one of the "People". Yes, that is their name. They are so busy being superior to everyone that no one ever realizes that they aren't very creative. They're amazed that Thorby is so smart and now he quickly moves up the ranks. However, the captain of the vessel feels guilty because Thorby is obviously destined for greatness. So he turns him over to the inter-galatic army. They discover that Thorby is a long lost heir from Earth and off we go to part 3!<br /><br />After a lot of conspiracy and complicated jargon, Thorby discovers that part of his new empire is...wait for it...seriously...running the slave trade! How ironic. Thorby vows to clean it up and the book ends.<br /><br />When I finished the last page, I literally checked to make sure that some of the book hadn't accidentally gotten cut out. I couldn't believe it was such a weird ending.<br /><br />Also, Thorby had no sexual interest in women. People kept trying to match make him up to girls and he kept getting uncomfortable. Not because he was gay, but because he kept acting like he was six. I just thought that was an odd trait. You'd think he'd at least have been interested in sex, but nothing.<br /><br />However, it was an easy read and everyone ended up happy, except the bad guys, but they're not supposed to. So I guess I'll give it two stars.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6889708432552820940">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7324880-ruth?shelf=scifi-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">scifi-fantasy</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer872688900">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5462675057234057838">Citizen of the Galaxy was the first scifi I ever read. In elementary school I simply read everything available but in middle school the contents of the school library changed. Suddenly there were baby romances for girls and westerns and war novels for boys. The sexism didn't especially bother me -- eading books from the other side of the line was the least of things I got teased about -- none of the stories were particularly good. I could guess what was going to happen long before the end. Borin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5462675057234057838">Citizen of the Galaxy was the first scifi I ever read. In elementary school I simply read everything available but in middle school the contents of the school library changed. Suddenly there were baby romances for girls and westerns and war novels for boys. The sexism didn't especially bother me -- eading books from the other side of the line was the least of things I got teased about -- none of the stories were particularly good. I could guess what was going to happen long before the end. Boring!!!<br /><br />A substitute teacher-librarian saved me. She handed me CotG, and reading became fun again. It wasn't all that different from other stories -- street kid gets lucky AND works hard and makes good, but the setting was different. Life didn't have to be as I was living it, there were alternative possibilities. I devoured scifi as fast as I could find them.<br /><br />I have to say that rereading this book as an adult was disappointing. It was the first YA book I had ever read as an adult and I hadn't realized what a difference there was. Still, I have to rate it as I first read it, as the book that changed my reading preferences forever. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5462675057234057838">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_173124743">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/173124743" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4962899-tim" class="left imgcol" title="Tim"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1297414703p2/4962899.jpg" alt="Tim" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/173124743?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 06, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4962899-tim" class="user" title="Tim">Tim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10508738-citizen-of-the-galaxy" title="MP3 Book 9781433240898" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Young adult sci-fi fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4962899-tim?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4962899-tim?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4962899-tim?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer173124743">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1557486276536093859">This book starts out with a beggar buying a young slave boy for very little money and might set certain expectations in one's mind, but the beggar isn't all he seems to be and it's one of the best things that could happen to the boy.<br /><br />This is one of Heinlein's better juvenile novels. It really breaks down into three distinct parts. The first two are excellent, but the third (and final) part comes across as rushed and kind of thrown together. It's a problem with many of his novels, but most (includ</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1557486276536093859">This book starts out with a beggar buying a young slave boy for very little money and might set certain expectations in one's mind, but the beggar isn't all he seems to be and it's one of the best things that could happen to the boy.<br /><br />This is one of Heinlein's better juvenile novels. It really breaks down into three distinct parts. The first two are excellent, but the third (and final) part comes across as rushed and kind of thrown together. It's a problem with many of his novels, but most (including this one) are good enough before this to make up for the poor ending. <br /><br />While books like <a title="The Star Beast by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175328.The_Star_Beast">The Star Beast</a> and <a title="Red Planet by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50843.Red_Planet">Red Planet</a> are light adventure stories this is a more serious book that's better suited for older children. It deals with more adult themes (like slavery) and Thorby (our protagonist) has more important decisions to make than the protagonists in those other books. <br /><br />So, I'd recommend that younger readers might want to start with another book (such as the two I've mentioned or <a title="Have Space Suit-Will Travel by Robert A. Heinlein" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20417.Have_Space_Suit_Will_Travel">Have Space Suit-Will Travel</a>) this is an excellent book for a truly young adult audience.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1557486276536093859">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/62749844?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 09, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13338-ori-fienberg" class="user" title="Ori Fienberg">Ori Fienberg</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13338-ori-fienberg?shelf=adolescent-phase-makers" class="actionLinkLite">adolescent-phase-makers</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13338-ori-fienberg?shelf=good-yarns" class="actionLinkLite">good-yarns</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer62749844">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2961933397632849777">I have a friend who contends that this is the greatest sci-fi book for young adults ever published. While I certainly agree that this is a fine book for that (and all) ages, particularly as it may be a useful teaching tool for certain history, social studies, and civics classes, I still think "Ender's Game" takes the cake.<br /><br />The characters are well thought out and the plot is solid. I think there could be more galaxy gallivanting, maybe a few more episodes for young Thorby, but at the same time the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2961933397632849777">I have a friend who contends that this is the greatest sci-fi book for young adults ever published. While I certainly agree that this is a fine book for that (and all) ages, particularly as it may be a useful teaching tool for certain history, social studies, and civics classes, I still think "Ender's Game" takes the cake.<br /><br />The characters are well thought out and the plot is solid. I think there could be more galaxy gallivanting, maybe a few more episodes for young Thorby, but at the same time the plot is fairly tight, and that feeling may just be the yearning I get with every book that I enjoy: I wish there was more. Of course Robert Heinlein did write another 45 or so books, and the other two of his that I've read "Starship Troopers" and "Tunnel in the Sky" I've also enjoyed. Orson Scott Card has written many novels as well, but I was never able to get into "Speaker For the Dead," and I feel as though my politics and particular sociological interests align somewhat more closely with those that seep into Heinlein novels. <br /><br />Certainly I feel I should read some more Heinlein. Any suggestions?</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7414160639360004608">4.5 stars<br /><br />Robert A. Heinlein’s best books are those he wrote for kids, and Citizen of the Galaxy is one of the best of those. Originally serialized in Astounding Science Fiction in 1957, this is an anthropological adventure story with strong libertarian and anti-slavery themes.<br /><br />We first meet Thorby, a young belligerent orphaned slave boy, as he has just landed on an unfamiliar planet and is on the auction block.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText7414160639360004608">4.5 stars<br /><br />Robert A. Heinlein’s best books are those he wrote for kids, and Citizen of the Galaxy is one of the best of those. Originally serialized in Astounding Science Fiction in 1957, this is an anthropological adventure story with strong libertarian and anti-slavery themes.<br /><br />We first meet Thorby, a young belligerent orphaned slave boy, as he has just landed on an unfamiliar planet and is on the auction block. Nobody wants him — he’s too feisty — but he is eventually sold for a pittance to Baslim, a man who appears to be a crippled beggar. Despite Baslim’s kind treatment, it takes a while for Thorby to warm up, but once he does he discovers that Baslim is more than he seems. Under Baslim’s tutorage, Thorby becomes an accomplished and very well educated beggar.<br /><br />Eventually Thorby must leave his new home, but the knowledge and connections he received from Baslim ...         rated it

          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">male teenagers between 13-19</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5346734-linda?shelf=family-audio-books" class="actionLinkLite">family-audio-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5346734-linda?shelf=youth" class="actionLinkLite">youth</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5346734-linda?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5346734-linda?shelf=action-adventure-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">action-adventure-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1442486924">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6119153608089964166"><em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> was divided into three distinct parts, each of which held my interest, but the first two parts were better than the third. I was involved until the very last word, but when it was over, I was disappointed. The conclusion was lackluster. The book abruptly ended with very little wrap up. I’ve seen this before with Heinlein, and I don’t like it when he gives up on an ending.<br /><br />I might have been tempted to give <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> five stars, if Heinlein had bothered to end it.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6119153608089964166"><em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> was divided into three distinct parts, each of which held my interest, but the first two parts were better than the third. I was involved until the very last word, but when it was over, I was disappointed. The conclusion was lackluster. The book abruptly ended with very little wrap up. I’ve seen this before with Heinlein, and I don’t like it when he gives up on an ending.<br /><br />I might have been tempted to give <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> five stars, if Heinlein had bothered to end it. The first two parts are very interesting and there are endless possibilities for part three. Considering the rushed conclusion, I am rating it 3½. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12275581060266898826">By far my favorite Heinlein. The only re-read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_592082669">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/592082669" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4959290-brad-wheeler" class="left imgcol" title="Brad Wheeler"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1298932053p2/4959290.jpg" alt="Brad Wheeler" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/592082669?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 16, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4959290-brad-wheeler" class="user" title="Brad Wheeler">Brad Wheeler</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10508738-citizen-of-the-galaxy" title="MP3 Book 9781433240898" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=sf-with-good-aliens" class="actionLinkLite">sf-with-good-aliens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=audiobooks" class="actionLinkLite">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=classic-sff" class="actionLinkLite">classic-sff</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=hard-sf" class="actionLinkLite">hard-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer592082669">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer408009069949704024">I have to say, I'm envious of how simple and unadorned Heinlein's writing is. His writing is not flowery or poetic or stylish, it just kind of "is." And yet, his stories move alone briskly and are full of believable characters and places. Frankly, I'd give a lot to be able to write as effortlessly as Heinlein seemed to.<br /><br />So, the book itself. I liked it. The story takes place over three distinct settings in three distinct--and yet entirely human and believable--cultures. The main character, Thorby,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText408009069949704024">I have to say, I'm envious of how simple and unadorned Heinlein's writing is. His writing is not flowery or poetic or stylish, it just kind of "is." And yet, his stories move alone briskly and are full of believable characters and places. Frankly, I'd give a lot to be able to write as effortlessly as Heinlein seemed to.<br /><br />So, the book itself. I liked it. The story takes place over three distinct settings in three distinct--and yet entirely human and believable--cultures. The main character, Thorby, a slave with no memory of his past, is purchased by an old beggar who raises him as his son. The beggar is not who he seems to be, of course, and the story goes on from there. It never becomes epic and there's never any galaxy-shaking action, but the way that the beginning of the story and the lessons Thorby learns there inform the rest of the novel lead to a conclusion that follows entirely naturally from the beginning of the story. It's something more authors, myself included, could learn.<br /><br />Heinlein writes believable cultures, like I mentioned, but I particularly love how he tackles the way that culture informs language, and vice versa. The Free Traders, for instance, have complex names for familial relationships because the peace is kept on their ships through strict--if informal--enforcement of family hierarchies. It's a subtle touch, and Heinlein keeps the infodumps mercifully short, but I loved it nonetheless.<br /><br />My only real complaints about the novel were that it contained some typical golden-age-sf weirdness, like an emphasis on perfect memory techniques and theraputic hypnosis that seem charmingly quaint and are hard to take seriously. A few of the characters in the last act are largely straw men, which is particularly disappointing after all the life characters had in the earlier sections. The final resolution is told, rather than shown, and what should be a climactic showdown is avoided entirely. <br /><br />I'm probably the last person to read this book, but if you by some chance haven't picked up a copy but enjoy classic science fiction, you'll definitely enjoy this one.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="408009069949704024">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_137862748">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137862748" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4730347-suzanne" class="left imgcol" title="Suzanne"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Suzanne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/137862748?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 03, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4730347-suzanne" class="user" title="Suzanne">Suzanne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer137862748'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer137862748">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9815911421536551457">This was always one of my favorite Heinlein juveniles. I zipped through it in a day after reading <em>Grumbles from the Grave</em>. It surprised me to find it was so short; somehow I remembered it as being longer.<br /><br />Despite the fact that I have always enjoyed reading this book and this time through was no exception, I found myself wincing at the following:<br /><br /><em>[Baslim] had never liked hypnosis, even for therapy; he had an almost religious concept of the dignity of the individual; hypnotizing another person did n</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9815911421536551457">This was always one of my favorite Heinlein juveniles. I zipped through it in a day after reading <em>Grumbles from the Grave</em>. It surprised me to find it was so short; somehow I remembered it as being longer.<br /><br />Despite the fact that I have always enjoyed reading this book and this time through was no exception, I found myself wincing at the following:<br /><br /><em>[Baslim] had never liked hypnosis, even for therapy; he had an almost religious concept of the dignity of the individual; hypnotizing another person did not fit his basic evaluations.<br /><br />But this was an emergency.</em><br /><br />I can remember when California's legislature topped its tuition increases at some low figure--four percent?--except in cases of emergency; the University of California subsequently declared an emergency every year.<br /><br />Also bothersome was Margaret the anthropologist's explanation of the Free People to Thorby on page 186. In the context of trading women between ships, she says, "[I]f men were boss, girl-swapping would be slavery; as it is, it's a girl's big chance."<br /><br />She has a fine argument for her position. This may be the reason why I never really noticed how inconsistent she was on page 251 when she lists all the ship's rules and asks Thorby:<br /><br /><em>"Thorby, what sort of people have so little freedom? Slaves? Can you think of a better word?"<br /><br />"But we can't be sold!"<br /><br />"Slavery has often existed where slaves were never bought and sold, but simply inherited. As in Sisu. Thorby, being a slave means having someone as your master, with no hope of changing it. You slaves who call yourselves the 'People' can't even hope for manumission."</em><br /><br />The point of the latter speech, of course, is that <em>Thorby</em> is in danger of being made a slave again. We are not really concerned with the fact that by Margaret's new argument, girl-swapping probably <em>is</em> slavery (though it is also still a girl's big chance). <br /><br />That having been said, I would disagree with Margaret that the Free People are slaves simply because they live in a rule-ridden environment. Margaret's definition is an extreme one and opens up the question, I think, of how many social rules create slavery. The Free People have many rules, but if they had only half as many, would Margaret still consider them slaves? How about a quarter as many? If the Free People only had a single rule--let us say "clothes must be worn in the presence of the Captain"--would they be slaves? Does Heinlein want to tell us that we're all slaves because we all live in a society with some taboos? If being a slave means "going to bed when your mother tells you to," are we trivializing slavery?<br /><br />Still on the topic of slavery, there is another giant hole in the novel--and this one did trouble me, much more than Margaret's seeming inconsistency, because it affects the entire plot. Why does the slave trade exist? What do slaves do that machines couldn't do better? Consider that Heinlein is seriously suggesting that slave traders might carry a recalcitrant child from one planet to another on a space ship for the purpose of selling his labor. Heinlein also lets us know that space ships are very expensive--that's why the Rudbek corporations have so much money and why it's hard for the Free People to split. Is the profit from slave-trading really enough to pay for all these ships?<br /><br />The Rudbek corporations are involved in selling ships to slavers. Are they doing so at the market price? At a below-market price, because they want to support slavery? Why would the Rudbek corps want to support slavery? If they're doing so at the market price, does Heinlein think the market ought to be regulated? In general, Heinlein's always seemed pretty free-market in his philosophy to me. Or is this another situation where Baslim's "But this was an emergency!" excuse is to be applied--the market should never be regulated, except when it involves slavery (and probably Communists and atomic weapons testing and anything else Heinlein really, really didn't like). <br /><br />We see a lot of poor people and slum life on Jubbul; we don't see slaves doing whatever it was that Heinlein thought slaves were bought to do. At one point, we get to see slaves carrying a rich lady's sedan chair. That's about it. <br /><br />I also found myself wondering this time through why Thorby wasn't murdered along with his parents. Why would assassins let one of their victims escape? And why is Baslim, the very competent spy, using <em>his real name</em> while undercover? <br /><br />But on the whole, I turned pages rapidly and rooted for Thorby throughout. I've always liked this novel in spite of the holes. My favorite part is always the end, when Thorby gets involved in corporate intrigue. As a child, the idea that control and ownership were different was fascinating to me, and it's one of the things that I took away from this novel.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9815911421536551457">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12290562300404645243">SUZANNE, .you inspired me to comment on your review by your question: why would they have slavery when machines could do the work cheaper an faster? W</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12290562300404645243">SUZANNE, .you inspired me to comment on your review by your question: why would they have slavery when machines could do the work cheaper an faster? Well all you have to do is read the occasional articles in the new media (here in the U.S.) in which people from some middle eastern countries are arrested for slavery, having brought their domestic servants here and used them as slaves. Why would they do such a thing? The perpetrators are generally well to do, and are quite capable of taking care of themselves, yet they hold the domestic prisoners. Why? We're talking about a different mindset here, wherein the economics aren't so important to the perpetrators. And that is one of Heinlein's points.</span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/376343419" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3727861-andrea" class="left imgcol" title="Andrea"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442528074p2/3727861.jpg" alt="Andrea" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/376343419?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 27, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3727861-andrea" class="user" title="Andrea">Andrea</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=1900s-1950s-era" class="actionLinkLite">1900s-1950s-era</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=spaceships" class="actionLinkLite">spaceships</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=space-adventure" class="actionLinkLite">space-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer376343419">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13660042622660906301">Heinlein here has moved outward from Earth, to a full intergalactic dispersal of Earth's inhabitants among multiple political groupings - from an Earth 'hegemony' to a slave trading Empire. The story follows Thorby, captured by slavers at a very young age, mistreated continually until bought by a beggar with complex secrets, who raises him to be a pillar of honesty and intellectual endeavour.<br /><br />The strongest section of the book is the beginning, where we are thrown deeply into Thorby's situation. T</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13660042622660906301">Heinlein here has moved outward from Earth, to a full intergalactic dispersal of Earth's inhabitants among multiple political groupings - from an Earth 'hegemony' to a slave trading Empire. The story follows Thorby, captured by slavers at a very young age, mistreated continually until bought by a beggar with complex secrets, who raises him to be a pillar of honesty and intellectual endeavour.<br /><br />The strongest section of the book is the beginning, where we are thrown deeply into Thorby's situation. There is some loss of traction in the middle section of the story, when Thorby joins a space-trading 'clan', and the resolution of the tale is pretty much dogged endurance of legal battles and right-feeling caution while sticking to one's guns, ultimately leading to the importance of living up to the responsibility of one's lot in life. It's not a bad story, but doesn't live up to the power of its opening.<br /><br />Character voice is also rather odd. This slave boy raised in the far future, experiencing multiple cultures, speaks with the Oh Shucks, Gee Whiz of a stereotypical Iowa farmboy.<br /><br />The treatment of women has improved in that we now have more of a range presented, from a 'wise old anthropologist' to girls who can do mathematics, to a level-headed socialite. There's still a handful of dismissive statements about the incapability of girls, but they are shown being competent repeatedly. They're also shown as primarily adjuncts to men, their purpose in life revolving around 'catching' and then supporting a suitable husband.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13660042622660906301">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_280347305">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/280347305" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7821634-larou" class="left imgcol" title="Larou"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1329869108p2/7821634.jpg" alt="Larou" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/280347305?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 19, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7821634-larou" class="user" title="Larou">Larou</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16689.Citizen_of_the_Galaxy" title="Paperback 9781416505525" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7821634-larou?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7821634-larou?shelf=2012-02" class="actionLinkLite">2012-02</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer280347305">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5035956354095123566">I have always had somewhat ambivalent feelings about Robert A. Heinlein – well, maybe not always: If I’m not mistaken I encountered him first when I was still a child, in German translation, and most likely abridged; those weren’t even paperbacks but the brochure format that was and I think still is popular in Germany for all kinds of pulp literature and which I used to devour by the dozens back in the day, and through all genres – Science Fiction, Romance, Crime, Western… I read them all.[retur</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5035956354095123566">I have always had somewhat ambivalent feelings about Robert A. Heinlein – well, maybe not always: If I’m not mistaken I encountered him first when I was still a child, in German translation, and most likely abridged; those weren’t even paperbacks but the brochure format that was and I think still is popular in Germany for all kinds of pulp literature and which I used to devour by the dozens back in the day, and through all genres – Science Fiction, Romance, Crime, Western… I read them all.[return][return]Anyway – Heinlein. As I grew up, and moved from his juveniles to his later work, my youthful enthusiasm for novels like <em>Have Spacesuit Will Travel</em> began to wane somewhat. Seen with a more critical eye brought on with greater maturity (or so I hope), it becomes hard to overlook that many of his political views are not exactly enlightened, that there is some weird stuff going on with gender relations, and that there is overall way too much sermonising on whatever subject happened to catch Heinlein’s fancy at the moment – <em>Stranger in a Strange Land</em>, to cite but one example, is pretty much one long sermon from beginning to end.[return][return]On the other hand… there just is something about Heinlein that grips you and you won’t let go, that draws you in and keeps you reading and turning pages even as you groan at yet another sermon and roll your eyes at yet another awkward fornication scene. I’m still trying to figure out what constitutes that appeal for me, but I strongly suspect that it has do with his narrative voice. I don’t think anybody is going to accuse Heinlein of writing literary fiction, and yet I have only recently seen the prevalence of voice of plot singled out as the trait that seperates literary fiction from genre literature. Based on the writings of Heinlein and some others I tend to disagree with that assessment, because while his works are very much genre, there also is a dinstinct style to them, a certain tone to their narrative voice, a unique lilt to the rhythm of their prose that are unmistakeably his.[return][return]That tone seems to go best with first person narrative (as indeed most of his novels are), and maybe that is the reason why it did not appear very pronounced in <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em>, as that novel is told entirely in the third person. Which would make for a weaker Heinlein novel, except that there is hardly any of his trademark sermonising either, and it being a juvenile, no sex at all. It’s almost like his good side and his bad side were somehow interdependent on each other, and you couldn’t have one without the other. And if you tone down on one, you’ll water down the other and end up with some kind of middle-of-the-road Heinlein, which is basically what <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> is – it is not his best, but not his worst either, in fact it probably would make a good introduction for people new to Heinlein to find out whether they can stomach him at all, or even for people who otherwise dislike his works.[return][return]It is an entertaining romp, even if there is not really much happening – it’s bascially a classical Bildungsroman, with the hero wandering around, seeing places and getting to know people, all of which makes him grow as a person and finally become fully himself. There is of course an SFnal element, in that that he also encounters a variety of different societies in space and on planets - and I think this is something that makes <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> stand out among other sci-fi novels of the period, in so far as back in the day (1957, to be precise) there was not that much Science Fiction around that placed its emphasis on anthropology and sociology rather than on the hard sciences.[return][return]And a final point in which I find this novel noteworthy is in clearing up Heinlein’s attitude towards women which is a persistently recurring debate. While none of them are primary characters, there are a lot of female secondary characters around – and not a single one of them is the simpering, helpless wifey sort that would conform to the period stereotype, but without exception they are all extremely intelligent and highly competent persons. I don’t think anybody who reads <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em> with an open mind will in good conscience be able to call Heinlein a misogynist. However, while there is nothing wrong with his female characters per se, the way he depicts their role in future society and in relationships appears to me to be deeply problematic – it seems even in the future, women cannot hold a position of power by themselves, but need to be married to a powerful man to achieve anything, with the male being in public view and the female using their relationship to manipulate and pull strings in the background to exert any influence. That is a very 19th century attitude, and seems strangely anachronistic for a future society. But then, they still have slavery, too, and the struggle to abolish it is <em>Citizen of the Galaxy</em>’s main plot point… except that there is no Hegemonic Guard to fight for female emancipation.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5035956354095123566">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11910891726316026364">Robert A. Heinlein’s best books are those he wrote for kids, and Citizen of the Galaxy is one of the best of those.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText11910891726316026364">Robert A. Heinlein’s best books are those he wrote for kids, and Citizen of the Galaxy is one of the best of those. Originally serialized in Astounding Science Fiction in 1957, this is an anthropological adventure story with strong libertarian and anti-slavery themes.<br /><br />We first meet Thorby, a young belligerent orphaned slave boy, as he has just landed on an unfamiliar planet and is on the auction block. Nobody wants him — he’s too feisty — but he is eventually sold for a pittance to Baslim, a man who appears to be a crippled beggar. Despite Baslim’s kind treatment, it takes a while for Thorby to warm up, but once he does he discovers that Baslim is more than he seems. Under Baslim’s tutorage, Thorby becomes an accomplished and very well educated beggar.<br /><br />Eventually Thorby must leave his new home, but the knowledge and connections he received from Baslim ...         rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>
